We need to produce an improved proof. The problem: For n≥3 integer, determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:

1. For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.

2. Exactly k of the n lines are sunny (a line is sunny if it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0).

Interpretation: We have a set of n lines (distinct). For each grid point (a,b) with a,b positive integers (≥1) and a+b ≤ n+1, that point must be on at least one line. The lines themselves are not required to be distinct? Actually "n distinct lines" means they are distinct lines. Exactly k of them are sunny (i.e., not parallel to any of the three forbidden directions). The rest are non-sunny, meaning they are parallel to either the x-axis (horizontal), y-axis (vertical), or the line x+y=0 (slope -1). So non-sunny lines have slope 0, ∞, or -1.

We need to determine all possible k (depending on n) for which such a configuration exists.

We need to combine the strongest ideas from the candidate solution (which only gave partial progress and a conjecture) and fix gaps. Since the candidate solution is insufficient, we must produce a complete rigorous proof.

We should first analyze the problem thoroughly.

Let's restate:

- Let L be a set of n distinct lines in the plane.
- For each positive integer pair (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, we have (a,b) ∈ ∪_{ℓ∈L} ℓ.
- Exactly k lines in L are sunny: i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So sunny lines have slopes different from 0, ∞, -1.

Goal: For given n≥3, find all nonnegative integers k for which such a set exists.

Observations:

- The points (a,b) with a,b ≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1 form a triangular grid of points. There are (n)(n+1)/2? Actually number of positive integer pairs (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1: For s = a+b from 2 to n+1, there are s-1 pairs (a,b) with a,b≥1. So total = sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = sum_{t=1}^{n} t = n(n+1)/2. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 grid points.

- Each line can cover many of these points. But lines are distinct.

- Sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1. They can be any other slope. They can be of any orientation, but note that if a line is not parallel to the three forbidden directions, it is sunny. However, the definition says "sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0." So a line that is parallel to one of those is non-sunny. So sunny lines can have any slope except 0, ∞, -1.

Let's systematically consider the maximum coverage of a sunny line in the triangular grid of size n (points with a+b ≤ n+1). We need to produce a rigorous proof.

Let's first define the set of points: P_n = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a ≥ 1, b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

We have n distinct lines ℓ_1,...,ℓ_n. For each point in P_n, it lies on at least one line. Exactly k lines are sunny.

Goal: Find all k (as function of n) for which such a configuration exists.

Observations: For a line ℓ, define its type: horizontal (slope 0), vertical (slope ∞), diagonal (slope -1), or other (sunny). Non-sunny lines are exactly those of the three types.

We need to consider constraints from the points.

Idea: Count the total number of incidences (point-line pairs) in two ways. Let I = ∑_{ℓ∈L} |ℓ ∩ P_n|. Then I ≥ |P_n| = n(n+1)/2, since each point is on at least one line. Also, I = ∑_{ℓ∈L} |ℓ ∩ P_n|.

We can bound |ℓ ∩ P_n| for each line based on its type. For a sunny line, the maximum number of points from P_n it can contain? Possibly it can contain up to something. For non-sunny lines, the maximum is: horizontal y=c: points with that y-coordinate, a from 1 to n+1-c, so number = n+1-c (provided c≥1 and n+1-c≥1). So max when c=1 gives n points (since n+1-1 = n). But for n≥3, the maximum is n? Actually for n=3, y=1 covers 3 points; n=4, y=1 covers 4 points; n=5, y=1 covers 5 points. So maximum is n. Similarly vertical x=c: points with that x, b from 1 to n+1-c, so also max n when c=1. For slope -1 line x+y = d: points with that sum, a from 1 to d-1, b = d-a, so number = d-1, for d from 2 to n+1, max when d=n+1 gives n points (since n+1-1 = n). So non-sunny lines can cover at most n points (for n≥3, actually for n=3, max is 3; n=4, max is 4; etc.). So each non-sunny line can cover at most n points.

For a sunny line, what is the maximum number of points from P_n it can contain? It can be any slope except 0,∞,-1. We need to consider the maximum number of points of the form (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 that lie on a line of slope m ≠ 0,∞,-1. This is a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's denote the line as y = mx + c, with m not 0,∞,-1. The points have integer coordinates. The line can contain at most something like O(n) but could be as high as n? For example, slope 1 line y=x: points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (n,n) but note a+b≤n+1: (n,n) gives sum=2n, which is > n+1 for n≥2. So only points with a=b and a+b≤n+1, i.e., a≤(n+1)/2. So the maximum number of points on y=x in P_n is floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, floor(4/2)=2; n=4, floor(5/2)=2; n=5, floor(6/2)=3. So for n=5, y=x covers 3 points. So max can be about n/2. For slope 2, y=2x: points with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, b=2a. Then a+2a≤n+1 => 3a≤n+1 => a≤(n+1)/3, so number ≈ n/3. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line is at most floor((n+1)/2) (achieved by slope 1) but could be larger for other slopes? Slope 1 gives floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 3. Slope 0.5 gives y=0.5x? Not integer points? Actually slope 1/2: y = (1/2)x + c; for integer points, we need x even etc. So number might be similar. Possibly if the line passes through many points with small a+b, but the constraint a+b≤n+1 limits the range. For a line of slope m, the maximum number of integer points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 is at most something like O(n). In general, for a given slope m (not 0,∞,-1), the number of points from P_n on a line y=mx+c is at most something. The maximum over all sunny lines is achieved by slope 1? But we need I ≥ n(n+1)/2. So we have:

k * floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

This is a necessary condition for existence. For n≥4, floor((n+1)/2) is at most (n+1)/2. So we have:

k * ((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Compute left: k*(n+1)/2 + n(n-k) = k*(n+1)/2 + n^2 - nk = n^2 + k*(n+1)/2 - nk = n^2 + k*(n+1 - 2n)/2 = n^2 + k*(1 - n)/2 = n^2 - k*(n-1)/2.

So we need n^2 - k*(n-1)/2 ≥ n(n+1)/2 = (n^2+n)/2.

Thus n^2 - k*(n-1)/2 ≥ (n^2+n)/2 => Multiply 2: 2n^2 - k(n-1) ≥ n^2+n => n^2 - k(n-1) ≥ n => n^2 - n ≥ k(n-1) => n(n-1) ≥ k(n-1) => n ≥ k (since n-1>0). So we get k ≤ n. That's trivial. But we need a stronger condition? Actually we used an upper bound for sunny line coverage (floor((n+1)/2)) which is less than n, but the inequality gave k ≤ n. That's always true. So the necessary condition from coverage is not restrictive enough to rule out k=2 for n≥4. We need a more refined argument.

Given the candidate solution only gave partial progress (k=0,1,3 for n≥4, and also they suspect k=2 impossible). We need to produce a complete rigorous proof.

Let's formalize.

Define P_n = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

We have n lines ℓ_1,...,ℓ_n, each distinct. Let S be the set of sunny lines (type: not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0). Let N = n - k (non-sunny lines).

For each point (a,b) ∈ P_n, it lies on at least one line.

Goal: Determine all k (as function of n) for which such a set exists.

Observations:

- Non-sunny lines are exactly those of slope 0, ∞, or -1. So they are horizontal, vertical, or diagonal (x+y = constant). These three families are mutually disjoint (except possibly at intersection points, but lines from different families can intersect, and that's fine).

- For a given line ℓ, define its type.

We need to use combinatorial arguments about the points.

Idea: Count incidences I = ∑_{ℓ} |ℓ ∩ P_n|. Since each point is on at least one line, I ≥ |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

Also, I = ∑_{ℓ∈sunny} |ℓ∩P_n| + ∑_{ℓ∈non-sunny} |ℓ∩P_n|.

We can bound |ℓ∩P_n| for non-sunny lines: For a horizontal line y = c (c ≥ 1), the points are (a, c) with a ≥ 1, a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1-c. So |ℓ∩P_n| = n+1-c, provided n+1-c ≥ 1. So max when c=1 gives n points (since n+1-1 = n). Similarly vertical x = c gives n points. For diagonal x+y = d (d ≥ 2), points are (a, d-a) with a ≥ 1, d-a ≥ 1 => 1 ≤ a ≤ d-1, and also a+d-a = d ≤ n+1 => d ≤ n+1. So number of points = d-1, max when d=n+1 gives n points. So each non-sunny line can contain at most n points. Also, note that for a given non-sunny line, the number of points it contains is exactly n when it is the "extreme" line: e.g., y=1 (c=1) gives n points; x=1 gives n points; x+y=n+1 gives n points.

For a sunny line ℓ, let its slope m ≠ 0, ∞, -1. Then ℓ is of the form y = mx + c or x = my + c (but since m is not 0 or ∞, we can use slope-intercept). The number of integer points (a,b) with a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 lying on ℓ is at most floor((n+1)/2). More generally, we can prove:

Claim: For any line ℓ with slope m ≠ 0, ∞, -1, the number of points of P_n on ℓ is at most floor((n+1)/2). Moreover, equality can be achieved only by lines of slope 1 (or -1? but -1 is non-sunny). Actually slope 1 gives floor((n+1)/2) points: (1,1),(2,2),..., (floor((n+1)/2), floor((n+1)/2)). For slope 1, the condition a+b≤n+1 becomes 2a≤n+1 => a≤floor((n+1)/2). So number = floor((n+1)/2). For other slopes, the number is smaller. So we can use a general bound: For a sunny line ℓ, |ℓ∩P_n| ≤ floor((n+1)/2). For n≥4, floor((n+1)/2) ≤ n/2+1/2, which is less than n.

Now, total incidences I = ∑_{sunny} |ℓ∩P_n| + ∑_{non-sunny} |ℓ∩P_n| ≤ k * floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k) * n.

Thus we have:

n(n+1)/2 ≤ I ≤ k * floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k) * n.

This gives a necessary condition:

k * floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k) * n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: n^2 - k(n-1)/2? Actually we computed earlier: For n even, floor((n+1)/2) = n/2 (since n+1 odd, floor = (n+1)/2? Wait: if n is even, say n=4, n+1=5, floor(5/2)=2, which is n/2? n/2=2 yes. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3 = n/2? n/2=3 yes. So for n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2. For n odd, n=5, floor(6/2)=3 = (n+1)/2? (5+1)/2=3 yes. So general: floor((n+1)/2) = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. But we can handle separately.

Let's denote t = floor((n+1)/2). Then inequality:

k t + (n-k)n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => n^2 - k n + k t ≥ n^2/2 + n/2 => Multiply 2: 2n^2 - 2k n + 2k t ≥ n^2 + n => n^2 - 2k n + 2k t ≥ n => n^2 - n ≥ 2k n - 2k t = 2k(n - t) => k ≤ (n^2 - n) / (2(n - t)). Note that n - t =? For n even, t = n/2, so n - t = n/2, then RHS = (n(n-1))/(2*(n/2)) = (n(n-1))/(n) = n-1. So k ≤ n-1. For n odd, t = (n+1)/2, so n - t = n - (n+1)/2 = (n-1)/2, then RHS = (n(n-1))/(2*((n-1)/2)) = (n(n-1))/(n-1) = n. So k ≤ n. So this gives k ≤ n for odd n, which is always true. For even n, we get k ≤ n-1. So for n even, k cannot be n (i.e., all lines sunny). For n odd, the bound does not rule out k=n. But we need to check if k=n is possible for odd n>3? For n=3, it works; for n=5, we argued it seems impossible because we would need 5 sunny lines covering 15 points. But the bound from coverage is not restrictive enough; we need a more precise counting.

We need to prove that for n≥4, k=2 is impossible, and also maybe k=0,1,3 are possible for all n≥3? But for n=3, k=3 works; for n=4, k=3 works; for n=5, we need to check k=3 works. Likely yes. Also, we need to check if k=0,1,3 are possible for all n≥3. For n=3, we have constructions for 0,1,3. For n=4, we have constructions for 0,1,3. For n=5, we can attempt to construct for k=3. Use non-sunny lines: maybe x=1 and y=1, covering 9 points, leaving 6 points. We need 3 sunny lines to cover those 6 points. As we saw, the leftover set is (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). Are there 3 sunny lines covering these? Let's try to find lines of slope 1 that cover 3 points each? The points on y=x: (2,2),(3,3) only 2; on y=x+1: (2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in set; on y=x+2: (2,4) only 1; on y=x-1: (3,2) only 1; so no. Could we use lines of slope 2? y=2x: (2,4) only; y=2x-1: (2,3)? (2,3): 3=4-1 yes, so (2,3) and (3,5) no; so 1 point; y=2x-2: (3,4) no; etc. So not many. Could we use lines of slope 1/2? y=0.5x+? For integer points, x must be even. (2,2) gives c=1; (4,2) gives c=0; so line through (2,2) and (4,2) is horizontal, non-sunny; through (2,3) and (4,4) but (4,4) not in set; etc. So not.

Maybe we can choose different non-sunny lines. Perhaps use sum=5 and sum=6? That leaves (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). For k=3, we need 3 sunny lines to cover these 6 points. Is there a set of 3 sunny lines covering these? Now, we need to cover these 6 points with 3 sunny lines. We already gave a construction for n=4 and n=5 that works. For general n, we can use the same pattern: define three sunny lines:

L1: through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) (slope = (2-1)/((n-1)-(n-2)) = 1/1 = 1, sunny). That line also passes through (n,3)? Not in set, but (n,3) not present. So it covers (n-2,1) and (n-1,2).

L2: through (n-2,2) and (n,1) (slope = (1-2)/(n-(n-2)) = -1/2, sunny). That covers (n-2,2) and (n,1).

L3: through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) (slope = (1-3)/((n-1)-(n-2)) = -2/1 = -2, sunny). That covers (n-2,3) and (n-1,1).

These three lines are distinct, each sunny, and they cover all 6 points. Check: (n-2,1) on L1; (n-1,2) on L1; (n-2,2) on L2; (n,1) on L2; (n-2,3) on L3; (n-1,1) on L3. All covered. So we have a valid construction for any n≥4. Thus, k=3 works for all n≥3.

Now, we need to prove impossibility for k=2.

We need to show: For n≥4, there is no set of n distinct lines such that exactly 2 are sunny and all points (a,b) with a+b≤n+1 are covered.

Approach: Use counting argument with incidences.

Let L be the set of n lines, exactly 2 sunny. Let N = n-2 non-sunny lines. These non-sunny lines are each of slope 0, ∞, or -1.

We want to derive a contradiction.

We can consider the points of P_n. Each point must be on at least one line. We can count incidences I = ∑_ℓ |ℓ ∩ P_n|.

We have I ≥ |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

We can also bound I from above. For a sunny line ℓ, |ℓ ∩ P_n| is at most something. For n≥4, the maximum points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). For n even, floor((n+1)/2)=n/2; for n odd, floor((n+1)/2)=(n+1)/2.

Let t = floor((n+1)/2). Then each sunny line can cover at most t points. The non-sunny lines can cover at most n points each. So I ≤ 2t + (n-2)n.

Thus we need:

n(n+1)/2 ≤ 2t + (n-2)n.

Simplify:

n(n+1)/2 ≤ 2t + n^2 - 2n => Multiply 2: n(n+1) ≤ 4t + 2n^2 - 4n => n^2 + n ≤ 4t + 2n^2 - 4n => 0 ≤ 4t + n^2 -5n => 4t ≥ 5n - n^2? Actually bring: 0 ≤ 4t + n^2 -5n => 4t ≥ 5n - n^2. For n≥5, 5n - n^2 ≤ 0, so inequality holds. For n=4, t=2, 4*2=8, RHS 5*4-16=20-16=4, so 8≥4 holds. So this bound does not rule out k=2.

We need a stronger bound, possibly considering that the sunny lines cannot simultaneously cover many points because they are "confined" to a certain shape. Maybe we can use a combinatorial geometry argument about the points that are not covered by non-sunny lines.

Let’s denote the non-sunny lines. They are of three types: horizontal, vertical, diagonal. Each such line covers points that are "aligned" along a certain direction. The points of P_n are in a triangular arrangement. The non-sunny lines, if placed optimally, could cover many points. But we need to show that if we have only 2 sunny lines, then the total coverage is insufficient.

Another idea: Consider the set of points that are not on any of the non-sunny lines. These points must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. So the union of the two sunny lines must contain all points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines. That set is P_n minus the union of the non-sunny lines. If we can bound the size of that set from below by something, and show that two sunny lines cannot cover that many points, we get contradiction.

But the non-sunny lines could be chosen to cover as many points as possible. However, we have n-2 non-sunny lines. We need to find a lower bound on the number of points not covered by any non-sunny line, given that the non-sunny lines are each of the three families. We can try to maximize the coverage by non-sunny lines, and then see that the uncovered points are at least something, and that two sunny lines cannot cover that many.

Let’s attempt to compute the maximum number of points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line can cover at most n points, the maximum total coverage is (n-2)n. However, there may be overlaps; the maximum distinct points covered is at most (n-2)n, but we need to see if we can achieve that without overlapping. If we choose all lines from different families that are disjoint (e.g., horizontal lines y=1,2,...,n-2? But each horizontal line y=c covers points with x from 1 to n+1-c, so they are not disjoint; they can overlap? Actually horizontal lines y=c and y=c' with c≠c' are disjoint because they have different y-coordinates. So they are disjoint. So we could take n-2 horizontal lines, each covering n points? Wait, horizontal line y=c covers points with a from 1 to n+1-c. So the number of points on y=c is n+1-c, which is less than n for c>1. The maximum is n when c=1. So if we take horizontal lines y=1,...,n-2, the total distinct points covered = sum_{c=1}^{n-2} (n+1-c) = sum_{t=3}^{n} t = (n+3)(n-2)/2? Actually sum_{c=1}^{n-2} (n+1-c) = sum_{i=3}^{n} i = (n+3)(n-2)/2. For n=5, that's 8*3/2=12; but total points=15; so leftover=3 points. So n-2 horizontal lines cover 12 points, leaving 3 uncovered. Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. As we argued, each sunny line can cover at most? Possibly 3 points? For n=5, t=3, so a sunny line can cover up to 3 points. So it's possible. But we need to consider that the sunny lines might also cover points already covered by horizontal lines, but that's fine. So this doesn't give contradiction.

Thus, we need a more refined argument that uses the geometry of the grid.

Perhaps we can use the following idea: For n≥4, consider the points (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n) (on the left boundary). These points are all on the vertical line x=1, which is non-sunny. Similarly, points (1,1), (2,1), ..., (n,1) are on y=1. The diagonal points (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1) are on x+y=n+1. These three families of lines cover many points. In our construction for k=3, we used x=1 and the diagonal, and then 3 sunny lines covered the rest. Given the complexity, it's clear that k=2 is impossible for n=4. We need to prove that for n≥4, k=2 cannot work. The candidate solution's partial progress stated that k=2 is impossible for n≥4. We need to provide a rigorous proof.

Let's try to prove impossibility for k=2.

We have n lines, exactly 2 sunny. Let the non-sunny lines be of three types: horizontal (H), vertical (V), diagonal (D). Their equations: y = c, x = c, x+y = d.

We need to cover all points of P_n.

Observation: For any point (a,b) ∈ P_n, it lies on at most one line of each type? Actually a point can lie on at most one horizontal line (y=c), at most one vertical line (x=c), and at most one diagonal line (x+y=d). So the total number of lines that can pass through a given point is at most 3, but we have exactly 2 sunny lines, which are not from these families.

We can try to count the total number of incidences I = ∑_{ℓ} |ℓ ∩ P_n|.

We know I ≥ |P_n| = N.

We also have an upper bound: For each sunny line ℓ, |ℓ ∩ P_n| ≤ t, where t = floor((n+1)/2) (max points on a sunny line). For each non-sunny line ℓ, |ℓ ∩ P_n| ≤ n. So I ≤ 2t + (n-2)n.

Thus we need N ≤ 2t + (n-2)n.

For n=4, N=10, t=2, RHS=4+8=12, holds. For n=5, N=15, t=3, RHS=6+15=21, holds. For n=6, N=21, t=3, RHS=6+24=30, holds. So this bound is not enough.

We need a stronger bound, possibly considering that the sunny lines cannot cover many points because they are "confined" to a certain shape, and also the non-sunny lines might not be able to cover all points if placed optimally.

Another approach: Consider the "boundary" of P_n. The points with x=1 or y=1 or x+y=n+1 are on the three families. These points form a "triangular" boundary. The number of points on the boundary is 3n-3? Actually for n≥3, the number of points with x=1 is n (since b=1..n), with y=1 is n, with x+y=n+1 is n, but they overlap at (1,n) and (n,1) and (1,1)? Let's compute: points on x=1: (1,b) for b=1..n. On y=1: (a,1) for a=1..n. On diagonal: (a,n+1-a) for a=1..n. Overlaps: (1,1) is on x=1 and y=1; (1,n) is on x=1 and diagonal; (n,1) is on y=1 and diagonal; also (1,1) is on y=1, (1,n) is on diagonal, (n,1) is on diagonal, (1,1) is on x=1. So distinct points on boundary = n + n + n - 3 = 3n-3. That's the number of points that are not interior? For n=4, 3*4-3=9, and interior points = 10-9=1 (point (2,2)? Actually (2,2) is interior? Check: (2,2) sum=4≤5, but x=2,y=2; boundary includes (2,1),(1,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1),(1,4),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) etc. So (2,2) is interior. For n=5, 3*5-3=12, interior=3, which are (2,2),(2,3),(3,2)? Actually (3,3) sum=6≤6, but (3,3) is on diagonal? x+y=6, so diagonal line includes (3,3) when n=5? n+1=6, so (3,3) is on x+y=6, so boundary. So interior points for n=5: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That matches.

Thus, the set of points that are not on the boundary is the interior, of size N - (3n-3) = n(n+1)/2 - (3n-3) = (n^2+n-6n+6)/2 = (n^2-5n+6)/2 = (n-2)(n-3)/2. For n=4, that's 2*1/2=1 interior point. For n=5, 3*2/2=3 interior points. For n=6, 4*3/2=6 interior points. So the interior points are those with x≥2, y≥2, and a+b≤n+1? Actually x≥2, y≥2, and x+y≤n+1. So they form a smaller triangular grid of size (n-2) in both directions? For n=6, interior points: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). That's 6 points.

Now, the points on the boundary are covered by lines from the three families. Since we have n-2 non-sunny lines, we can choose them to cover the boundary points. However, note that the boundary points are on the lines x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1. We have n-2 lines, so we can cover at most n-2 of these boundary lines? Actually we can choose, say, all vertical lines x=1,2,...,n-2 (these are non-sunny). But x=1 covers n boundary points; x=2 covers points with x=2, but the boundary points with x=2 are (2,1) and (2,? ) Actually (2,1) is on y=1, (2,? ) if n=6, (2,5) is on diagonal? (2,5) sum=7>6 no, so (2,1) is boundary, (2,? ) (2,2) interior, (2,3) interior? Actually (2,3) sum=5≤6, but x=2,y=3; is that on boundary? No, x=2 not 1, y=3 not 1, x+y=5 not 7; so interior. So x=2 covers only the point (2,1) on the boundary (since (2,? )? (2,1) only). So the vertical lines x=2..n-2 might cover only a few boundary points. Similarly, horizontal lines y=2..n-2 might cover only (1,y) boundary? Actually y=2 covers (1,2) and (2,2) interior? (1,2) boundary, (2,2) interior. So each horizontal line y=c for c=2..n-2 covers (1,c) boundary point. So if we use vertical lines x=1..n-2 and horizontal lines y=1..n-2, that's 2(n-2) lines, but we only have n-2 lines total, so we can't use both.

Thus, we need to allocate the n-2 non-sunny lines to cover as many boundary points as possible, and also cover the interior points with the 2 sunny lines.

The interior points (size (n-2)(n-3)/2) must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. Each sunny line can contain at most t points from P_n. But can a sunny line contain many interior points? For n≥4, the interior points are all with x≥2,y≥2,x+y≤n+1. The maximum number of interior points on a sunny line is something. For slope 1 line y=x, it passes through interior points when 2a≤n+1 => a≤(n+1)/2, but also a≥2, so number = max(0, floor((n+1)/2)-1). (3,3) is on diagonal? x+y=6, so boundary; (2,2) interior, (3,3) is boundary? Actually for n=5, n+1=6, so (3,3) is on x+y=6, so boundary; so interior points on y=x: a=2 only, 1 point. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3, so a=2,3 -> (2,2) interior, (3,3) interior? (3,3) sum=6≤7, but x+y=6, boundary? n+1=7, so (3,3) is not on x+y=7, so interior; actually boundary includes x+y=7, not 6. So (3,3) interior. So 2 points. So a sunny line of slope 1 can cover at most floor((n+1)/2)-1 interior points? For n=4, that's 2-1=1; n=5, 3-1=2? But we found only 1 interior point on y=x for n=5? Let's check: interior points for n=5: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). y=x passes through (2,2) only, (3,3) is not interior because (3,3) is on diagonal? Actually for n=5, x+y≤6, so (3,3) is on the boundary? x+y=6, so it's on the diagonal line x+y=n+1, so yes boundary. So the interior points for n=5 are those with x+y≤5? Now, each sunny line can cover at most t interior points. For n≥4, t = floor((n+1)/2). So there are multiple sunny pairs among interior points. Could a sunny line contain 3 interior points? Possibly if the three points are collinear on a sunny line. Check: y=x passes through (2,2),(3,3) only; (2,3),(3,3) horizontal; (2,4),(3,3) slope -1; (2,2),(4,2) horizontal; (2,3),(4,2) slope -0.5; (2,4),(4,2) slope -1; (3,2),(4,2) horizontal; (2,3),(3,2) slope -1; (2,2),(3,2) horizontal; (2,2),(2,4) vertical; (2,3),(4,2) slope -0.5; (2,4),(3,2) slope -2; (3,2),(3,3) vertical? Actually (3,2) and (3,3): vertical x=3, non-sunny. So no line contains 3 interior points. So maximum points on a sunny line among interior points is 2. For n=6, interior points=6, so 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 interior points if they are disjoint? But they might overlap; the maximum distinct points covered by 2 sunny lines is at most 2+2=4 (since each line can contain at most 2 interior points). So with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 4 interior points, leaving 2 uncovered. Thus k=2 impossible for n=6 as well.

General pattern: For n≥4, the interior points (size I_n = (n-2)(n-3)/2) must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. Each sunny line can contain at most some number M of interior points. We need to show that M ≤ something such that 2M < I_n for n≥4. But we saw that for n=4, I_4=1, M=1, 2*1=2≥1, but the configuration fails because of boundary points? Actually we need to consider both interior and boundary. For n=4, we had interior=1, boundary=9, but we had 2 non-sunny lines to cover boundary; they can cover at most 7 boundary points (x=1 and y=1 cover 7), leaving (2,3) and (3,2) uncovered. Those are interior? No, they are boundary? For n=4, points (2,3) and (3,2) are on diagonal? x+y=5, so yes boundary. So after using x=1,y=1, we have 2 boundary points left. Those need to be covered by sunny lines. But sunny lines can also cover interior points. However, we have 2 sunny lines; they can cover at most 2 points among the left points? Actually they can cover at most 2 points if each covers at most 1, but they also need to cover interior point (2,2). So total 3 points to cover, but only 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points if each covers at most 1. But perhaps a sunny line can cover 2 points among the leftover? For n=4, leftover points after x=1,y=1 are (2,3),(3,2),(2,2). Can a sunny line cover two of these? (2,2)-(2,3) vertical; (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal; (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1; all non-sunny. So no. So each sunny line covers at most 1 point, so 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, leaving 1 uncovered. So impossible.

For n=5, interior points=3, boundary=12, but we have 3 non-sunny lines. Could we choose non-sunny lines to cover all boundary points? The boundary points are on x=1,y=1,x+y=6. With 3 lines, we could choose x=1 (covers 5 points), y=1 (covers 5 points, overlap (1,1)), and x+y=6 (covers 5 points, overlaps with x=1 at (1,5) and y=1 at (5,1)). That's 5+5+5-3=12 points, covering all boundary points. Then interior points (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) need to be covered by 2 sunny lines. As before, each sunny line can cover at most 1 interior point, so 2 sunny lines cover at most 2, leaving 1 uncovered. So impossible.

For n=6, interior points=6, boundary=15, with 4 non-sunny lines. Could we choose non-sunny lines to cover all boundary points? The boundary points are on x=1,y=1,x+y=7. With 4 lines, we could choose x=1 (6 points), y=1 (6 points), x+y=7 (6 points), and maybe one more line? But we have 4 lines total. If we take x=1,y=1,x+y=7, that's 3 lines, covering 6+6+6-3=15 points, covering all boundary points. Then we have 1 more non-sunny line? Actually we need 4 non-sunny lines. So we can add, say, x=2 or y=2 or x+y=6? But we already have x=1,y=1,x+y=7. Adding x=2 covers points (2,1)-(2,5) but (2,5) sum=7, so (2,5) already on diagonal? Actually (2,5) sum=7, so it's on x+y=7, already covered. So new points from x=2: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) interior. So that would add 3 interior points. Then we still have interior points left. So we need to cover all interior points with the 2 sunny lines. The interior points are (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). Can 2 sunny lines cover all 6? As we argued, each sunny line can contain at most 2 interior points (since no line contains 3 interior points). So 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 interior points. So at least 2 interior points would be uncovered. So impossible.

Thus, for n≥4, the interior points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because the maximum number of interior points on a sunny line is at most something like floor(n/2)-1? For n=6, that's 2; for n=7, interior points=10, max per sunny line? We need to compute the maximum number of interior points on a sunny line. Let's analyze more generally.

Given n, interior points are those with x≥2,y≥2, and x+y≤n. They form a right triangle of size (n-2) in both directions. The points have integer coordinates. A line ℓ (not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) can contain at most some number of these points. We need to find an upper bound for the number of interior points on a sunny line. For a line of slope m, we can count the number of integer points (a,b) with a≥2,b≥2, a+b≤n that satisfy b = ma + c. This is a linear Diophantine problem. The maximum over all m and c is something like O(n). For slope 1, we have points (a,a) with a from 2 to floor(n/2). So number = max(0, floor(n/2)-1). For slope 2, we have points (a,2a+c) with 2a+c ≥2, a+2a+c ≤ n => 3a+c ≤ n. For given c, the number is about n/3. So the maximum is achieved by slope 1, giving about n/2 points. But note that for interior points, a+b≤n, so for slope 1, a≤n/2. So number of interior points on y=x is about n/2. For n=7, that's 3.5 -> floor(7/2)=3, so a=2,3,4? But a=4 gives (4,4) sum=8>7, so only a=2,3 give (2,2),(3,3) interior? Check: interior points for n=7 are those with x+y≤7. So (2,2) sum=4≤7, (3,3) sum=6≤7, (4,4) sum=8>7, so only 2 points. So the formula floor(n/2)-1 gives 3-1=2, correct. For n=8, interior points: a=2..4 gives (2,2),(3,3),(4,4) sum=8≤8, so 3 points. So max interior points per sunny line is about n/2? Actually for n=8, floor(8/2)-1=4-1=3, which is about n/2 - 1? n/2=4, minus 1=3. So the maximum is floor(n/2)-1. For n=6, that's 3-1=2, which matches. So in general, the maximum number of interior points on a sunny line is at most floor(n/2)-1.

But wait, for n=5, floor(5/2)-1=2-1=1, which matches that the only sunny line with interior points is y=x giving (2,2) only. For n=4, floor(4/2)-1=2-1=1, (2,2) only. So the maximum is M = floor(n/2)-1.

Now, the total number of interior points is I = (n-2)(n-3)/2. For n≥4, we can compare 2M and I.

2M = 2(floor(n/2)-1) = 2 floor(n/2) - 2.

For n even, say n=2k, then 2M = 2k - 2 = n-2. I = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2 = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2 = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2 = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2. For k=2, n=4: I=1, n-2=2, 2≥1. For k=3, n=6: I=6, n-2=4, 4<6. So for n≥6 even, I > n-2. For n odd, n=2k+1, then 2M = 2k - 2 = n-3. I = (2k-1)(2k-2)/2 = (2k-1)(k-1) = 2k^2-3k+1. For k=2, n=5: I=3, n-3=2, 2<3. For k=3, n=7: I=10, n-3=4, 4<10. So for n≥5 odd, I > n-3. So for n≥5, we have I > 2M, meaning that 2 sunny lines cannot cover all interior points if each sunny line can contain at most M interior points. For n=4, I=1, 2M=2≥1, so the interior points could be covered, but we also need to consider boundary points. For n=4, we have 2 non-sunny lines. Can we cover all boundary points with 2 non-sunny lines? The boundary points are 9 points. With 2 non-sunny lines, the maximum distinct points they can cover is at most 2n? Actually each non-sunny line can cover up to n points. So 2 lines cover at most 2n = 8 points. But we need to cover 9 boundary points. So it's impossible to cover all boundary points with 2 non-sunny lines. However, note that the boundary points are covered by the non-sunny lines, but the interior point might be covered by sunny lines as well. But the boundary points are on the three families; we have only 2 lines, so we can cover at most 2 lines from the families. The three families are x=1, y=1, x+y=5. With 2 lines, we can cover at most 2 of these lines, but each line covers many points. For example, using x=1 and y=1 covers 7 boundary points (as before). The remaining 2 boundary points (2,3) and (3,2) are on x+y=5. So we need to cover those with sunny lines. But then we also need to cover interior point (2,2). So that's 3 points to cover with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, impossible. So n=4 fails.

Thus, we can prove impossibility for all n≥4.

Now we also need to check if k=n is possible for n=3 only. That's included in k=3.

So the answer: For n≥3, the possible k are 0, 1, 3.

Now we should provide rigorous proofs for each case.

We'll structure the solution:

- Define P_n.
- Show that k=0 works: take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n. (All vertical, non-sunny). They cover all points because any point (a,b) has x=a, and a from 1 to n, so it lies on x=a line. (Need to ensure that for a given point, a is between 1 and n, yes.)
- Show that k=1 works: take lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n-2, and y=1. That's n-1 lines. x=1,...,n-2 are vertical (non-sunny). y=1 is horizontal (non-sunny). So we have n-1 non-sunny lines. Then we need one sunny line to cover the remaining points. The uncovered points are those with x≥n-1 and y≥2? Actually after using x=1..n-2, all points with x≤n-2 are covered. The points with x=n-1, n are not covered by vertical lines. Also, points with y=1 are covered by y=1 line. So uncovered points are those with x=n-1 or n, and y≥2. Also, note that (n-1,1) and (n,1) are covered by y=1 line. So uncovered set = { (n-1, y) : y from 2 to n+1-(n-1)=2? Actually a+b≤n+1: for a=n-1, b≤2, so only (n-1,2). For a=n, b≤1, but b≥2 gives none. So only point (n-1,2) is uncovered. That's one point. So we need a sunny line through (n-1,2). Choose, for example, line y = x - (n-3) (slope 1). This line passes through (n-1,2) and is sunny (slope 1 ≠ 0,∞,-1). So k=1 works.
- Show that k=3 works: use lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=n-3 (these are n-3 non-sunny lines). Also, use the diagonal line x+y=n+1 (non-sunny). That's (n-3)+1 = n-2 non-sunny lines. Then we need 3 sunny lines to cover the remaining points. The remaining points are those with x≥n-2 and y≥2? Actually after covering x=1..n-3, all points with x≤n-3 are covered. The points with x=n-2, n-1, n are not covered by vertical lines. Also, the diagonal x+y=n+1 covers points with that sum. The uncovered points are those with x≥n-2 and x+y≤n+1? But we have diagonal covering some of them. Let's compute: points with x=n-2: y from 1 to n+1-(n-2)=3, so (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3). Among these, (n-2,1) is on y=1? But y=1 not used; (n-2,1) is covered by? x=n-2 not vertical; y=1 not used; diagonal x+y=n+1? n-2+1=n-1≠n+1; so (n-2,1) is not covered by non-sunny lines. Similarly, (n-2,2),(n-2,3) are uncovered. Points with x=n-1: y from 1 to 2, so (n-1,1),(n-1,2). (n-1,1) uncovered, (n-1,2) uncovered. Points with x=n: y=1 only, (n,1) uncovered. So uncovered points = (n-2,1),(n-2,2),(n-2,3),(n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1). That's 6 points. Now we can cover them with 3 sunny lines as given: L1: through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) (slope 1, sunny). L2: through (n-2,2) and (n,1) (slope -1/2, sunny). L3: through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) (slope -2, sunny). These lines are distinct and sunny, covering all 6 points. So k=3 works.

Now prove that k=2 is impossible for n≥4.

We need to show that if exactly 2 lines are sunny, then the points of P_n cannot all be covered.

Proof: Suppose we have n lines, exactly 2 sunny. Then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny, each of slope 0, ∞, or -1.

Let I_n = (n-2)(n-3)/2 be the number of interior points (points with x≥2,y≥2,x+y≤n). These interior points are not on any of the three boundary families x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1? Actually they are not on x=1, y=1, and x+y≤n, so they are not on x+y=n+1 either because x+y≤n. So they are not on any of the three families. Therefore, they cannot be covered by the non-sunny lines. Hence, they must be covered by the 2 sunny lines.

Now, consider any sunny line ℓ. We claim that ℓ can contain at most floor(n/2)-1 interior points. Indeed, if ℓ has slope m≠0,∞,-1, then its equation is y = mx + c. For an interior point (a,b) with a≥2,b≥2,a+b≤n, we have b = ma + c. Since a+b≤n, we have a + ma + c ≤ n => (m+1)a ≤ n - c. Also, a≥2. So the number of a such that (a,ma+c) is interior is at most floor((n-c)/(m+1)) - 1 (since a≥2). The maximum over all m and c is attained when m=1, c=0? But then a ≤ n/2, and a≥2 gives at most floor(n/2)-1 points. For other slopes, the bound is smaller. So indeed, each sunny line contains at most M = floor(n/2)-1 interior points.

Thus, the total number of interior points covered by the 2 sunny lines is at most 2M. But we have I_n interior points, so we need 2M ≥ I_n.

Now, compute:

If n≥5, then for n=5, M=1, 2M=2, I_n=3, 2<3. For n=6, M=2, 2M=4, I_n=6, 4<6. For n=7, M=3? Actually floor(7/2)-1=3-1=2? Wait: floor(7/2)=3, minus 1 =2. So M=2, 2M=4, I_n=10, 4<10. For n=8, M=3, 2M=6, I_n=15, 6<15. In general, we can show that for n≥5, I_n > 2M. Because I_n = (n-2)(n-3)/2, and 2M = 2(floor(n/2)-1). For n even, n=2k: I_n = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2 = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2 = (2k-2)(2k-3)/2. For k≥3, I_n ≥ 6, and 2M = 2(k-1)=2k-2 = n-2. For n=6, I_n=6, n-2=4<6; for n=8, I_n=15, n-2=6<15; so I_n > n-2 for n≥6. For n odd, n=2k+1: I_n = (2k-1)(2k-2)/2 = (2k-1)(k-1) = 2k^2-3k+1. 2M = 2(k-1)=2k-2. For k≥2, I_n ≥ 3, and 2k^2-3k+1 > 2k-2 for k≥2? Check k=2: 8-6+1=3, 2k-2=2, 3>2; k=3: 18-9+1=10, 4, 10>4; so I_n > 2M. For n=5, I_n=3, 2M=2, holds. So for n≥5, I_n > 2M. Thus, the 2 sunny lines cannot cover all interior points. Therefore, k=2 is impossible for n≥5.

For n=4, we have I_n = (2)(1)/2=1. M = floor(4/2)-1=2-1=1, so 2M=2≥1. So the interior point could be covered. But we also need to consider the boundary points. The boundary points (points on x=1, y=1, x+y=5) total 9 points. The non-sunny lines are 2. Each non-sunny line can cover at most n=4 points. So the maximum number of distinct boundary points they can cover is at most 2*4=8. Since there are 9 boundary points, at least one boundary point is not covered by the non-sunny lines. Hence, it must be covered by a sunny line. But we have only 2 sunny lines, each of which can contain at most one interior point? Actually we need to also cover the interior point. However, we can try to see if a sunny line can cover a boundary point and an interior point. But note that the interior point is (2,2). The boundary points are on x=1, y=1, x+y=5. A sunny line through (2,2) might also pass through a boundary point. For n=4, the interior point is (2,2). The boundary points are: (1,1)-(1,4), (2,1),(3,1),(4,1), and (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) on diagonal. That's 9 points. Can we cover all these 9 boundary points with 2 non-sunny lines? With 2 lines, we can cover at most 8 points (since each line covers at most 4 points, but they might overlap). So at least 1 boundary point is left uncovered. That point must be covered by a sunny line. Also, the interior point (2,2) must be covered by a sunny line. So we need to cover at least 2 points (one boundary, one interior) with the 2 sunny lines. But can a single sunny line cover both? Possibly if the sunny line passes through (2,2) and that boundary point. But the boundary point is on one of the three families. Let's check if there is a sunny line through (2,2) and a boundary point. For example, boundary point (1,2) is on x=1. Line through (2,2) and (1,2) is horizontal y=2, non-sunny. Through (2,2) and (2,1) vertical x=2 non-sunny. Through (2,2) and (1,1) slope 1, sunny? (2,2) to (1,1) slope 1, sunny. But (1,1) is on boundary. So if we use that sunny line, it covers (2,2) and (1,1). Then we still need to cover the remaining boundary points with the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are 2. Can we cover all other boundary points? The remaining boundary points are (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) except (1,1) and (2,2) are covered. But (2,2) is interior; the boundary points left: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(2,3),(3,2). That's 8 points. Can we cover them with 2 non-sunny lines? Each non-sunny line can cover at most 4 points. We could use x=1 line, which covers (1,1)-(1,4) but (1,1) already covered, so new: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4). That's 3 points. Then use y=1 line, which covers (1,1)-(4,1) but (1,1) already, so new: (2,1),(3,1),(4,1). That's 3 points. That covers (1,2)-(1,4) and (2,1)-(4,1). That's 6 points. The remaining boundary points are (2,3) and (3,2). These are on diagonal x+y=5. They could be covered by the diagonal line x+y=5, but that would be a non-sunny line. We only have 2 non-sunny lines total. So we have used x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines, that's 2 lines. We still need to cover (2,3) and (3,2). They cannot be covered by the sunny lines because the only sunny line we have is through (2,2)-(1,1), which doesn't pass through (2,3) or (3,2). So (2,3) and (3,2) would be uncovered. Thus, not all points covered.

Could we choose different non-sunny lines? Maybe use x=1 and x+y=5 as non-sunny lines. Then we cover (1,1)-(1,4) and (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Overlap at (1,4). So distinct points: (1,1)-(1,4) 4 points, plus (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) 3 new points, total 7 points. The remaining points: (2,1),(3,1),(4,1) already? (4,1) covered; (2,1) and (3,1) not; also (2,2) interior; (3,2) covered; (2,3) covered; (1,2),(1,3) covered; (2,1),(3,1) left. That's 2 points. Also (2,2) interior. So we need to cover (2,2),(2,1),(3,1) with 2 sunny lines. As before, no sunny line can contain two of these, so each sunny line covers at most 1, total 2, leaving 1 uncovered. So impossible.

Thus, for n=4, k=2 is impossible.

Therefore, for all n≥4, k=2 is impossible.

Now we also need to check if k=n works for n>3. We already argued that all lines sunny cannot work because the interior points cannot be covered by n sunny lines? Actually if all lines are sunny, we have n sunny lines. Could they cover all points? For n=3, it works. For n=4, we have 4 sunny lines. The interior points are 1, but we have 4 sunny lines; they can cover that point. However, we need to cover all 10 points. Could 4 sunny lines cover all 10 points? Possibly, but we need to see if the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2)=2 for n=4, so 4 sunny lines can cover at most 4*2=8 points, but we need 10, so impossible. For n=5, max per sunny line=3, 5 lines cover at most 15 points, which equals total points, but we need to see if we can actually cover all points. The only way to get 15 distinct points is if each sunny line covers 3 points and they are disjoint. But as we argued, the only line that can cover 3 points is y=x (slope 1) giving 3 points? Actually for n=5, y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) is on diagonal? n+1=6, so x+y=6, so (3,3) is boundary; interior points are (2,2) only. So y=x covers 3 points, but two of them are boundary? Actually (1,1) boundary, (2,2) interior, (3,3) boundary. So that's 3 points. Other lines with slope 2 can cover 1 point; etc. So if we use 5 sunny lines, we need to cover 15 points. The only way to get 5 lines each covering 3 points is if they are all of slope 1, but then they would be lines y=x+c with c ranging from -? For n=5, the lines y=x+c with c from -4 to 4? But we only have 5 lines. The points of P_5 are partitioned into 9 such lines. So we cannot get 5 lines covering all 15 points. Thus, k=5 impossible. Similarly, for n≥6, max per sunny line is floor((n+1)/2) which is at most n/2+1/2, so n lines cover at most n*(n/2+1/2)=n^2/2 + n/2, but total points is about n^2/2, so it's possible in terms of counting. But we need to show that the points cannot be covered by n sunny lines. For n=6, total points=21, max per sunny line=3, so 6 lines cover at most 18 points, insufficient. For n=7, total points=28, max per sunny line=4, 7 lines cover at most 28 points, so possible in principle. But can we actually achieve coverage? We need to see if there exists a set of 7 sunny lines that partition P_7 into 7 sets of 4 points each. As argued, the only way to get 4 points on a sunny line is slope 1 line y=x passing through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) (all in P_7? (4,4) sum=8, n+1=8, so (4,4) is on diagonal, boundary; (1,1) boundary; (2,2) interior? Actually interior points for n=7 are those with x+y≤7; (2,2) sum=4≤7, interior; (3,3) sum=6≤7, interior; (4,4) sum=8>7, so (4,4) is not interior, but it's on the diagonal x+y=8, which is boundary. So that line contains 4 points, but two of them are boundary. So it's possible. Other lines with slope 1 give at most 2 points (for negative c). So to cover all 28 points with 7 lines, we would need lines with 4 points each. But there is only one such line (y=x) that gives 4 points. Other lines could have 3 points? For n=7, lines with slope 1 and c=1 give points (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) sum=7, interior? (3,4) sum=7≤7, interior; (1,2) boundary? x=1 boundary, y=2 not? (1,2) is on x=1 boundary; so that's 3 points, but (1,2) boundary, (2,3) interior, (3,4) interior? (3,4) sum=7, so interior. So that line covers 3 points. So we could have a combination. But the total number of points is 28; we need to cover 28 points with 7 lines. If we use y=x (4 points), then we need to cover the remaining 24 points with 6 lines. The maximum per line is 4 (if we use y=x-1? That would give (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (2,1) boundary, (3,2) interior, (4,3) interior? (4,3) sum=7 interior; so 3 points). So it's not possible to get 4 points from any other line. So the maximum total from 7 lines is at most 4 + 6*3 = 22, which is less than 28. So impossible. More generally, we can argue that for n≥4, the maximum total points on n sunny lines is less than N. This can be shown using an averaging argument: Each point on the grid is covered by a certain number of lines, but we can use the fact that the number of points on a line of slope m is at most floor((n+1)/2). For n≥5, we can compute that the total number of points is n(n+1)/2, and the maximum number of points that can be covered by k lines is k * floor((n+1)/2). For k=n, this gives n * floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, that's 5*3=15, equal to N, but we already saw that no set of 5 lines can cover all 15 points because the only way to get 15 distinct points is if each line covers 3 points and they are disjoint, but we cannot have 5 disjoint lines of slope 1 with 3 points each. For n=7, n*floor((n+1)/2)=7*4=28, equal to N. But can we have 7 disjoint lines each with 4 points? The points of P_7 are partitioned into 9 lines of slope 1. So we cannot have 7 disjoint lines each with 4 points because the maximum number of points on a line of slope 1 is 4, and there is only 1 such line. Other lines of slope 1 have at most 3 points. So the maximum total from 7 lines is at most 4 + 6*3 = 22 <28. So impossible. For n=9, floor((10)/2)=5, so n*5=45, N=45. But points of P_9 are partitioned into 2n+1? Actually we need to analyze generally: The points of P_n are partitioned by lines of slope 1 into (n+1) lines, with numbers: for c=0, n+1 points? Now we should provide rigorous proofs for the impossibility of k=2 for n≥4.

Let's write a clean proof:

Lemma: For n≥4, if exactly 2 lines are sunny, then the points of P_n cannot all be covered.

Proof: Suppose we have n lines, exactly 2 sunny. Then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny, each of slope 0, ∞, or -1.

Let I_n = (n-2)(n-3)/2 be the number of points with x≥2, y≥2, and x+y≤n. These points are not on any of the three families x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 (since x+y≤n < n+1). Therefore, they cannot be covered by the non-sunny lines. Hence, they must be covered by the 2 sunny lines.

Now, consider any sunny line ℓ. Since ℓ is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, its equation is y = mx + c with m≠0,∞,-1. For any interior point (a,b) on ℓ, we have b = ma + c. Because a+b≤n, we get a + ma + c ≤ n ⇒ (m+1)a ≤ n - c. Also, a≥2. Therefore, the number of a such that (a,ma+c) is interior is at most ⌊(n-c)/(m+1)⌋ - 1. The maximum over all m and c is attained when m=1, c=0, giving at most ⌊n/2⌋ - 1 points. For other slopes, the bound is smaller. Hence, each sunny line contains at most M = ⌊n/2⌋ - 1 interior points.

Thus, the 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2M interior points. But we have I_n interior points. Therefore, we must have 2M ≥ I_n.

Compute:

If n=4: M = ⌊4/2⌋ - 1 = 2-1=1, 2M=2, I_4=1, so 2≥1 holds. So the interior points could be covered.

If n=5: M = ⌊5/2⌋ - 1 = 2-1=1, 2M=2, I_5=3, 2<3. So impossible for n≥5.

If n=6: M = ⌊6/2⌋ - 1 = 3-1=2, 2M=4, I_6=6, 4<6. So impossible for n≥6.

For n≥5, we have I_n > 2M, as shown by direct computation. Therefore, k=2 is impossible for n≥5.

For n=4, we need to consider the boundary points separately. The total number of points is 10. The boundary points (points on x=1, y=1, x+y=5) are 9. The two non-sunny lines can cover at most 2*4=8 of these 9 boundary points. Hence, at least one boundary point is not covered by the non-sunny lines. That point must be covered by a sunny line. Also, the interior point (2,2) must be covered by a sunny line. So we need to cover at least 2 points (one boundary, one interior) with the 2 sunny lines. But can a single sunny line cover both? Possibly if the sunny line passes through (2,2) and that boundary point. However, we can argue that any line through (2,2) that also passes through a boundary point must be of slope 1 (through (1,1)) or slope -1 (through (3,1)? Actually check: boundary points are on x=1, y=1, or x+y=5. A line through (2,2) and a point on x=1: say (1,b). Then slope = (b-2)/(1-2) = 2-b. For this line to be sunny, we need slope ≠ 0,∞,-1. For b=1, slope=1, sunny; b=2, slope=0, horizontal; b=3, slope=-1, non-sunny; b=4, slope=-2, sunny; b=5, slope=-3, sunny. So there are possible sunny lines through (2,2) and a point on x=1 (e.g., (1,1) or (1,4) or (1,5)). Similarly, through y=1: (a,1) gives slope = (1-2)/(a-2) = -1/(a-2). For a=1, slope=1, sunny; a=3, slope=-1, non-sunny; a=4, slope=-1/2, sunny; a=5, slope=-1/3, sunny. So possible. Through diagonal x+y=5: say (a,5-a). Then slope = (5-a-2)/(a-2) = (3-a)/(a-2). For a=1, slope=2/(-1)=-2, sunny; a=3, slope=0/(-1)=0, horizontal; a=4, slope=-1/2, sunny; a=5, slope=-2/3, sunny. So possible. Thus, a sunny line through (2,2) could also pass through a boundary point. However, we also need to cover the remaining points with the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are 2. They must cover the rest of the boundary points and possibly some interior points. But note that after using one sunny line, we still have one sunny line left. The interior point is (2,2). If we use a sunny line that also covers a boundary point, then that boundary point is covered. The other boundary points (8 points) must be covered by the 2 non-sunny lines. But each non-sunny line can cover at most 4 points (since n=4). So 2 lines cover at most 8 points. So it's possible in principle to cover all 8 remaining boundary points. However, we also need to ensure that the non-sunny lines are chosen appropriately. The non-sunny lines are of the three families. We need to see if there exists a selection of 2 non-sunny lines that covers all 8 boundary points except the one covered by the sunny line. The boundary points are on x=1, y=1, x+y=5. The families are x=1, y=1, x+y=5. With 2 lines, we can cover at most 2 of these families. For example, if we use x=1 and y=1, they cover 7 boundary points (since they overlap at (1,1)). For n=6, interior points=6, each sunny line can cover at most 2 interior points (since M=2), so 2 sunny lines cover at most 4 interior points, but we have 6 interior points, so if k=2, impossible. For k=3, we have 3 sunny lines covering at most 6 interior points, so possible in principle. Could we have k=4? That would be 4 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Interior points=6, each sunny line covers at most 2 interior points, so 4 sunny lines cover at most 8 interior points, enough. But we also need to cover boundary points. The boundary points for n=6 are 15 points. The non-sunny lines are 2, each can cover up to 6 points, so at most 12 boundary points covered. Thus, at least 3 boundary points must be covered by sunny lines. But each sunny line can contain at most 2 boundary points? Possibly more? Need to analyze. This is getting complicated. However, we can argue that for n≥4, the only possible k are 0,1,3. Because we have constructions for those, and for any other k, we can use a counting argument based on the number of points on the grid that are not on the three boundary families. Actually the interior points (size I_n) must be covered by sunny lines. For a given k, the number of sunny lines is k. The maximum number of interior points that can be covered by k sunny lines is k * M, where M = floor(n/2)-1. So we need k * M ≥ I_n. For n=4, I=1, M=1, so k*1 ≥1 => k≥1. So k could be 1,2,3,... but we already have that k=2 impossible, and k=0 works, k=1 works, k=3 works. For n=5, I=3, M=1, so k*1 ≥3 => k≥3. So k must be at least 3. k=3 works, k=4? k=4 would satisfy 4*1≥3, so possible in that bound. But we need to consider that the sunny lines also need to cover boundary points. However, we can use a similar argument for boundary points. For n=5, boundary points are 12. The non-sunny lines are n-k = 5-k. For k=4, we have 1 non-sunny line. That line can cover at most 5 points. So the remaining 7 boundary points must be covered by the 4 sunny lines. Each sunny line can contain at most 3 boundary points? Actually we can bound the number of boundary points on a sunny line. For n=5, boundary points are on x=1, y=1, x+y=6. A line can intersect x=1 at most once, y=1 at most once, and x+y=6 at most once. So a sunny line can contain at most 3 boundary points. So 4 sunny lines can cover at most 12 boundary points. That's enough for 7. But we also have interior points. So it's not ruled out by that bound. However, we already have a construction for k=3, and we can argue that k=4 is impossible because the only way to cover all interior points with 4 sunny lines is to have each sunny line cover at most 1 interior point, but we need to cover 3 interior points, so we need at least 3 sunny lines. That's fine. But we also need to cover boundary points. However, we can try to see if there is a set of 4 sunny lines that covers all points. We already attempted for n=5 with k=4 and concluded it's impossible because the maximum points per line among the uncovered set is 2, and we have 10 points to cover with 4 lines, but each line can cover at most 2 points, so at most 8 points covered, insufficient. But is that always the case? For n=5, we computed that after using 1 non-sunny line (say x+y=6), the uncovered set had 10 points, and each sunny line could contain at most 2 of them? Actually we found that the largest number of uncovered points on a sunny line was 2 (e.g., y=x covers (1,1),(2,2) among uncovered; y=x+1 covers (1,2),(2,3); y=x-1 covers (2,1),(3,2); y=x+2 covers (1,3) only; etc. So indeed, each sunny line covers at most 2 uncovered points. So 4 lines cover at most 8 points, but we have 10 points. So impossible. This argument relies on the fact that the uncovered set after using the non-sunny lines is a set of points that have no line containing 3 of them. In general, for a given n, we can choose the non-sunny lines to cover as many points as possible. To show that k is impossible, we need to show that for any choice of n-k non-sunny lines, the remaining points cannot all be covered by k sunny lines. This is a combinatorial geometry problem that might be solved by a more general invariant.

Given the complexity, we can restrict to the cases we have proven: k=0,1,3 work, and k=2 is impossible for n≥4. For k>3, we need to show impossibility. But we can argue that if k≥4, then we have at least 4 sunny lines. Consider the interior points I_n. Each sunny line can contain at most M = floor(n/2)-1 interior points. So k*M ≥ I_n. For n=5, M=1, so k≥3. For k=4, we need to check if it's possible. But we already found it's impossible because the uncovered set after using non-sunny lines cannot be covered. However, we need to prove that for any n≥4, the only possible k are 0,1,3. We have proven that k=2 is impossible. For k≥4, we can use an argument based on the fact that the total number of points on the grid is N = n(n+1)/2. The number of points that can be covered by the non-sunny lines is at most (n-k)n (since each non-sunny line covers at most n points). The remaining points are at least N - (n-k)n = n(n+1)/2 - n(n-k) = n(n+1 - 2n + 2k)/2 = n(1 - n + 2k)/2 = n(2k - n + 1)/2. These points must be covered by the k sunny lines. The maximum number of points that k sunny lines can cover is k * floor((n+1)/2). So we need:

k * floor((n+1)/2) ≥ n(2k - n + 1)/2.

This is a necessary condition. For k≥4, we can test for n=5: floor(6/2)=3, LHS=3k, RHS=5(2k-5+1)/2 = 5(2k-4)/2 = 5(k-2). So 3k ≥ 5k-10 ⇒ 2k ≤ 10 ⇒ k ≤ 5. So k=4 satisfies (12≥10). So this condition doesn't rule out k=4. For n=6: floor(7/2)=3, LHS=3k, RHS=6(2k-6+1)/2 = 6(2k-5)/2 = 3(2k-5)=6k-15. So 3k ≥ 6k-15 ⇒ 3k ≤ 15 ⇒ k ≤ 5. So k=4 satisfies (12≥9). So this condition is not strong enough. We need a more refined argument.

Given the time, we can present a proof that for n≥4, k=2 is impossible, and also that k=n is impossible because the total number of points is too large relative to the maximum coverage by n lines (for n even, n* (n/2) = n^2/2, which is less than N? For n even, N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2, so n^2/2 < N, so n lines cannot cover all points if each covers at most n/2 points. But we need to consider that the maximum per line is floor((n+1)/2) = n/2 (since n even). So n lines cover at most n*(n/2)=n^2/2 < N. Thus, k=n impossible for even n. For odd n, N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. max per line = (n+1)/2, so n lines cover at most n*(n+1)/2 = N, so it's borderline. But we can argue that no set of n sunny lines can cover all points because the points are partitioned by lines of slope 1, and the largest lines have sizes at most floor(n/2)+1? Actually for odd n, n=2k+1, max per line = k+1, n lines cover at most n(k+1) = (2k+1)(k+1) = 2k^2+3k+1. Total points = (2k+1)(2k+2)/2 = (2k+1)(k+1) = 2k^2+3k+1. So it's equal. So it's possible in principle if each sunny line covers exactly k+1 points and they are disjoint. However, the points of the grid are not all on lines of slope 1; they are distributed across many slopes. But can we find n lines that are sunny and each contains k+1 points? The only way to get k+1 points on a line is to have slope 1 and c=0 (giving points (1,1)-(k+1,k+1) which includes some boundary points). For n=7, k+1=4, that line gives 4 points. For the remaining 6 points, we would need other lines with 4 points each, but there are no other lines with 4 points. So it's impossible. More generally, the points of P_n are partitioned by lines of slope 1 into at most n+1 lines, with the largest having size floor((n+1)/2). So the sum of the sizes of the k largest such lines is at most something. For n=2k+1, the k largest lines have sizes k+1, k, k-1, ..., 2? Actually we have lines: c=0: k+1 points; c=1: k points; c=-1: k points; c=2: k-1 points; c=-2: k-1; etc. So the sum of the k largest lines is at most (k+1) + k + k + (k-1) + (k-1) + ... but we only have k lines. The sum of the k largest lines is at most (k+1) + k + (k-1) + ... + 2? Actually we have two lines of size k (c=1 and c=-1) and then lines of size k-1, etc. So the sum is about 2k^2/2? This is messy. But we can use an averaging argument: Since the points are all on lines of slope 1, the number of points on each such line is at most floor((n+1)/2). For n=2k+1, that's k+1. The total number of points is (2k+1)(k+1). If we have n=2k+1 sunny lines, each covering at most k+1 points, then to cover all points, the average coverage per line must be (2k+1)(k+1)/(2k+1)=k+1. So each sunny line must cover exactly k+1 points, and they must be disjoint. Hence, the sunny lines must partition the points into sets of size k+1. But the points are already partitioned by lines of slope 1. So the sunny lines must be exactly those slope-1 lines. However, there are 2k+1 such lines? Actually for n=2k+1, the partition by slope-1 lines gives lines with sizes: k+1, k, k, k-1, k-1, ..., 2,2,1,1,... The number of lines with positive size is 2k+1? Let's see: For n=2k+1, n+1=2k+2, so c from - (k+1) to (k+1) but many zero. The lines with points: c=0: k+1 points; c=1: k points; c=-1: k points; c=2: k-1 points; c=-2: k-1 points; ... c=k: 1 point; c=-k: 1 point; c=k+1: 0; c=-k-1:0. So the number of lines with points is 2k+1 (from c=-k to c=k). The sum of their sizes is (2k+1)(k+1). So the partition consists of 2k+1 lines. Thus, the only way to partition the points into 2k+1 sets of size k+1 is to take each of these lines, but the lines have sizes k+1, k, k, k-1, etc., not all k+1. So it's impossible. Therefore, k=n is impossible for n≥5 odd as well.

Thus, we can conclude that for n≥4, the only possible k are 0,1,3.

Now we need to produce a complete rigorous solution.

We'll write:

Let P_n = {(a,b)∈ℕ^2: a≥1, b≥1, a+b≤n+1}.

We have n distinct lines ℓ_1,…,ℓ_n. Let S be the set of sunny lines (lines not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0). Let k = |S|.

We need to determine all k for which such a set exists.

First, we show that k=0 works: take lines x=1, x=2, …, x=n. All are vertical, non-sunny. Any point (a,b)∈P_n has x=a∈{1,…,n}, so lies on x=a. Thus all points covered.

k=1 works: take lines x=1, x=2, …, x=n-2 (n-2 lines), and y=1 (1 line). So we have n-1 non-sunny lines. The uncovered points are those with x≥n-1 and y≥2, but the only such point is (n-1,2) (since a+b≤n+1). Thus we need a sunny line through (n-1,2). Choose, for instance, the line y = x - (n-3), which has slope 1 and passes through (n-1,2). This line is sunny because slope 1 ≠ 0,∞,-1. Hence k=1 works.

k=3 works: take lines x=1, x=2, …, x=n-3 (n-3 lines) and the diagonal x+y=n+1 (1 line). These are n-2 non-sunny lines. The remaining points are: (n-2,1), (n-2,2), (n-2,3), (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1). We cover them with three sunny lines:
- L1: through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) (slope 1, sunny);
- L2: through (n-2,2) and (n,1) (slope -1/2, sunny);
- L3: through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) (slope -2, sunny).
These lines are distinct and sunny, and cover all six points. Thus k=3 works.

Now we prove that k=2 is impossible for n≥4.

Assume that exactly two lines are sunny. The other n-2 lines are non-sunny, each of slope 0, ∞, or -1.

Let I_n = (n-2)(n-3)/2 be the number of points with x≥2, y≥2, and x+y≤n. These points are not on any of the three families x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 (since x+y≤n < n+1). Hence they cannot be covered by the non-sunny lines. Therefore, they must be covered by the two sunny lines.

Now, consider any sunny line ℓ. Its equation is y = mx + c with m≠0,∞,-1. For any interior point (a,b) on ℓ, we have b = ma + c. Because a+b≤n, we get a + ma + c ≤ n ⇒ (m+1)a ≤ n - c. Also, a≥2. So the number of integer a such that (a,ma+c) is interior is at most ⌊(n-c)/(m+1)⌋ - 1. The maximum over all m,c is achieved when m=1, c=0, giving at most ⌊n/2⌋ - 1 points. Thus, each sunny line contains at most M = ⌊n/2⌋ - 1 interior points.

Hence, the two sunny lines can cover at most 2M interior points. Since we have I_n interior points, we must have 2M ≥ I_n.

For n=4: M = ⌊4/2⌋ - 1 = 2-1=1, 2M=2, I_4=1, so 2≥1 holds. So the interior points could be covered.

For n=5: M = ⌊5/2⌋ - 1 = 2-1=1, 2M=2, I_5=3, 2<3, impossible. For n=6: M = 3-1=2, 2M=4, I_6=6, 4<6, impossible. For n≥5, we have I_n > 2M, as can be checked directly: I_n = (n-2)(n-3)/2, and 2M = 2(⌊n/2⌋-1). For n=5, (3)(2)/2=3, 2(2-1)=2; for n=6, (4)(3)/2=6, 2(3-1)=4; for n=7, (5)(4)/2=10, 2(3-1)=4; etc. In general, for n≥5, I_n ≥ 3, and 2M ≤ n-2 (for even n) or n-3 (for odd n). For n≥5, I_n ≥ 3, and n-2 < (n-2)(n-3)/2 for n≥5? Check: (n-2)(n-3)/2 - (n-2) = (n-2)((n-3)/2 - 1) = (n-2)(n-5)/2 ≥ 0 for n≥5, equality only for n=5? For n=5, (3)(0)/2=0, so I=3, n-2=3, so equal. For n=5, we have 2M=2, I=3, so inequality. For n=6, I=6, n-2=4, 6>4. So indeed, I_n > 2M for n≥5. Thus, k=2 impossible for n≥5.
